<div id="review_1166002501" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1166002501" />
    <a title="Candi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3639005-candi"><img alt="Candi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585187464p2/3639005.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1166002501?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Candi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Candi" href="/user/show/3639005-candi">Candi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780671763800" href="/book/show/761667.True_Grit">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3639005-candi?shelf=westerns">westerns</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1166002501" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6139515080149975177"><i>"People do not give it credence that a fourteen-year-old girl could leave home and go off in the winter-time to avenge her father’s blood but it did not seem so strange then, although I will say it did not happen every day."</i><br/> <br/>This is exactly what Mattie Ross, now on my list of all-time favorite narrators/protagonists, sets out to do in this entertaining and adventure-filled western. The industrious Mattie hires Deputy Marshall Rooster Cogburn to search for her father’s killer in the formidable In</span>
  <span id="freeText6139515080149975177" style="display:none"><i>"People do not give it credence that a fourteen-year-old girl could leave home and go off in the winter-time to avenge her father’s blood but it did not seem so strange then, although I will say it did not happen every day."</i><br /> <br />This is exactly what Mattie Ross, now on my list of all-time favorite narrators/protagonists, sets out to do in this entertaining and adventure-filled western. The industrious Mattie hires Deputy Marshall Rooster Cogburn to search for her father’s killer in the formidable Indian Territory. Not to be left out of the action, Mattie insists on coming along for the ride. A thorn in both Rooster’s and Mattie’s sides, Texas Ranger LaBoeuf joins them as he has his own reasons for capturing this loathsome killer. Told with simple and straightforward prose, <b>True Grit</b> has wonderful moments of comical banter between Rooster, <i>"an old one-eyed jasper that was built along the lines of Grover Cleveland"</i>, the handsome but insolent LaBoeuf, and the spunky, intelligent Mattie Ross herself. Despite the discomfort of the journey, the uncertainty of the outcome, and the fear of the perils she encounters, Mattie refuses to give up and remains true to her goal of seeking justice. She haggles with the shrewdest of men and stands up to their relentless jabs about her age and gender as a hindrance to their mission. My only complaint with the book - I wish it could have been a bit longer so I could have relished it even more. It seemed to end way too fast for one I was enjoying so much!<br /><br />I can’t resist adding a couple of my favorite quotes here:<br /><br /><i>"I would not put a thief in my mouth to steal my brains."</i><br /><br /><i>"I have known some horses and a good many more pigs who I believe harbored evil intent in their hearts. I will go further and say all cats are wicked, though often useful. Who has not seen Satan in their sly faces? Some preachers will say, well that is superstitious claptrap. My answer is this: Preacher, go to your Bible and read Luke 8: 26-33."</i><br /><br />Don’t let the "western" genre turn you away from this great little book if you have any appreciation for a little piece of the American landscape, thrilling encounters with infamous outlaws, superb and cutting dialogue, and plucky female heroines. Thoroughly engaging throughout, <b>True Grit</b> seriously had me on the edge of my seat with the book gripped tightly in my hands during the dramatic climax. Can’t wait to re-watch the movie now!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6139515080149975177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1847572662" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1847572662" />
    <a title="Joe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61157160-joe"><img alt="Joe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569331508p2/61157160.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1847572662?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joe" href="/user/show/61157160-joe">Joe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1847572662" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3843654835846854550">After <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2956.The_Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn' title='The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn by Mark Twain' rel='nofollow noopener'> Huckleberry Finn</a> probably the single greatest novel of the American West. It's been adapted twice for the films. Neither captures what makes this a book so great: the stern, judgmental, archaic, hilarious voice of the narrator, Mattie Ross. The kind of novel you find yourself revisiting every couple years. I learned more about "voice" from this story than from anything else I've ever read.</span>
  <span id="freeText3843654835846854550" style="display:none">After <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2956.The_Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn" title="The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn by Mark Twain" rel="nofollow noopener"> Huckleberry Finn</a> probably the single greatest novel of the American West. It's been adapted twice for the films. Neither captures what makes this a book so great: the stern, judgmental, archaic, hilarious voice of the narrator, Mattie Ross. The kind of novel you find yourself revisiting every couple years. I learned more about "voice" from this story than from anything else I've ever read.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_841120469" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/841120469" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4086118-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567611029p2/4086118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/841120469?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4086118-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=american-west">american-west</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=oklahoma">oklahoma</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=arkansas">arkansas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer841120469" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5535041260715089772">A timeless classic of heroism, adventure, and coming of age that I found to be both thrilling and funny. The tale of how a 14-year old girl from Arkansas comes to avenge the murder of her father in 1878 is so pure and elegant, it can’t help but make you believe in the power of righteous determination to right the ills of the world. Told from the perspective of a straitlaced spinster decades later, we get a jaundiced eye on the human condition that puts human weakness and courage in a wonderful p</span>
  <span id="freeText5535041260715089772" style="display:none">A timeless classic of heroism, adventure, and coming of age that I found to be both thrilling and funny. The tale of how a 14-year old girl from Arkansas comes to avenge the murder of her father in 1878 is so pure and elegant, it can’t help but make you believe in the power of righteous determination to right the ills of the world. Told from the perspective of a straitlaced spinster decades later, we get a jaundiced eye on the human condition that puts human weakness and courage in a wonderful perspective. A personal hook for me was the setting of most of the action in eastern Ozarch parts of the future Oklahoma where I grew up.<br><br>On learning of her father’s death, Mattie leaves her grieving mother and siblings and sets out from Fort Sill, Arkansas, to collect his body. Upon learning the culprit Chaney has escaped into the lawless lands of Indian Territory, she sets about finding a man she can hire who is sufficiently mean and capable enough to find him and kill him if necessary. The man she carefully picks, Rooster Cogburn, is a middle-aged, one-eyed, fat alcoholic, but she believes he has the necessary “true grit” to get the job done. According to the sheriff: “He is a pitiless man, double-tough, and fear don’t enter into his thinking. He loves to pull a cork.” When she first encounters Cogburn, he is testifying in court against a captured murderer, where the defense he has to admit killing 23 bad guys in the course of his work going after felons hiding out in Indian Territory. She has found her man. <br><br>If you have seen the first movie based on the book, you can’t help but hold the aging John Wayne in your mind for the marshall. But there was a corny outlook to that version, and the more recent Coen brothers’ movie, with Jeff Bridges as Cogburn, is closer to Portis’ tone in the novel, its absurdist flavor and deadpan humor. See this <a href="http://www.gullcottageonline.com/Home_Video.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Web page</a> for an informative filmography (as an aside, the piece makes me wish for a Coen adaptation of DeWitt’s “The Sisters Brothers”, which appears to owe much to “True Grit”). An underground theme handled better in the second movie is the need for Cogburn to achieve some form of redemption for brutal deeds committed in the service with the infamous Quantrill Raiders during the Civil War. Mattie’s sense of Cogburn as a sort of surrogate for her lost father is also fulfilled better with that movie’s coverage of her efforts to track him down 25 years later.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1391277716i/8366780.jpg" width="400" height="256" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Portis on the set with Wayne during the making of the 1969 film version</i><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1391277716i/8366781.jpg" width="400" height="259" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Jeff Bridges and Hailee Steinfeld as Cogburn and Mattie in the 2010 movie</i><br> <br>The word that best characterizes Mattie in this story is relentless. In that way, she is in the same league with Larsson’s Salander, although she does not stray to the dark side of “Breaking Bad” like the latter. Early in the tale, she shrewdly negotiates a contract with Cogburn, forges a scheme to accompany him against his wishes, and wangles a horse and money out of a stock trader by threatening a lawsuit against him for liability in the theft of her father’s horse by Chaney. An ex-Texas ranger, LaBoeuf (pronounced “La Beef”), horns in on their quest, seeking the large bounty on Chaney’s head for killing a state senator. Mattie provokes him mercilessly over his mercenary ways, bragging, and dandified airs. As Chaney has joined up with the bandit gang of Lucky Ned Pepper, she gives in on having an extra man on the mission. But she gets her way on joining the two through implacability that garners their respect. <br><br>When asked to ID her father’s body, we see how readily she can set aside her grieving in favor of the job she needs to do:<br><i>“I said,”That is my father.” I stood there looking at him. What a waste! Tom Chaney would pay for this! I would not rest easy until that Louisiana cur was roasting and screaming in hell!<br>The Irishman said, “If ye would loike to kiss him it will be all roight.”<br>I said, “No, put a lid on it.”</i><br><br>This sample shows Portis’ mixture of plain prose in perfect declarative sentences and aptness in capturing local idioms he was steeped from his Arkansas origins. The full sentences used in dialogue is abnormal, but perhaps they could be seen as reflecting Mattie’s proper schooling as she writes from a distant future. Overall, I was totally charmed to delighted laughter with the speech he pulls out of his characters. For example, when Cogburn corners and exchanges gunfire with some thieves in a cabin, he demands to know who is inside and gets the answer: “A Methodist and a son of a bitch! … Keep riding!” I was particularly tickled with the floury speech of the stock trader near the beginning:. <br><i> “The killer has flown to the Territory and is now on the scout there.”<br>“This is what I heard.”<br>“He will find plenty of his own stamp there,” said he. “Birds of a feather. It is a sink of crime. Not a day goes by but there comes some new report of a farmer bludgeoned, a wife outraged, or a blameless traveler set upon and cut down in a sanguinary ambuscade. The civilizing arts of commerce do not flourish there.”</i><br><br>At the end of the 2004 edition of this 1968 book, Donna Tartt explains why it is a masterpiece for her and four generations of her family:<br><br><i>Mattie’s narrative tone is naive, didactic, hardheaded, and completely lacking in self-consciousness—and, at times unintentionally hilarious … A great part of True Grit’s charm is in Mattie’s blasé view of frontier America. Shootings, stabbings, and public hangings are recounted frankly and flatly, and often with rather less warmth than the political and personal opinions upon which Mattie digresses. She quotes scripture; she explains and gives advice to the reader; her observations are often overlaid with a decorative glaze of Sunday School piety. And her own very distinctive voice (blunt, unsentimental, yet salted with parlor platitudes) echoes throughout the reported speech of all the other characters—lawmen and outlaws alike—to richly comic effect.</i> <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="5535041260715089772" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3171041886" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3171041886" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13459162-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579803798p2/13459162.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3171041886?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/13459162-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13459162-edward?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13459162-edward?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13459162-edward?shelf=western">western</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3171041886" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11670805394074163141"><b>Check out my review for this fantastic book on Grimdark Magazine at: <a href='https://www.grimdarkmagazine.com/review-true-grit-by-charles-portis/' rel='nofollow noopener'>Grimdark Magazine</a></b> <br/><br/>***<br/><br/>So sad to hear the news. R.I.P. Charles Portis.<br/><br/>***<br/><br/>True Grit is a spectacular western that is full of heart with genuinely delightful characters that you instantly connect with. Charles Portis has created a wonderful tale of the west that will go down in my all time favourites.<br/><br/><b>“Fill your hand you son of a bitch!”</b><br/><br/>True Grit follows a young girl full of ‘grit’, setting out to bring her father’s murderer to just</span>
  <span id="freeText11670805394074163141" style="display:none"><b>Check out my review for this fantastic book on Grimdark Magazine at: <a href="https://www.grimdarkmagazine.com/review-true-grit-by-charles-portis/" rel="nofollow noopener">Grimdark Magazine</a></b> <br /><br />***<br /><br />So sad to hear the news. R.I.P. Charles Portis.<br /><br />***<br /><br />True Grit is a spectacular western that is full of heart with genuinely delightful characters that you instantly connect with. Charles Portis has created a wonderful tale of the west that will go down in my all time favourites.<br /><br /><b>“Fill your hand you son of a bitch!”</b><br /><br />True Grit follows a young girl full of ‘grit’, setting out to bring her father’s murderer to justice. Mattie Ross in the year 1878 is searching for Tom Chaney, the man who worked for her father then shot him. Mattie is 14 years old but is a fantastic character with as much gravitas as quippy remarks. She is straight to the point and one tough girl who does not messagings round and will not rest until Tom Chaney has either been shot by her dragoon pistol or hung from the noose.<br /><br />Mattie searches for a U.S. Marshall to assist her with the capture of Tom Chaney and eventually finds one in the rugged and experienced Rooster Cogburn, a Marshall who is known more for how many outlaws he has put bullets into rather than delivered alive. Cogburn’s relationship with Mattie is instantly enjoyable and they have some fantastic dialogue, Cogburn’s no-nonsense approach almost a match for Mattie’s hardy outlook. Once Mattie hires her whiskey-loving Marhsall she meets another man on Chaney’s trail - a Texas Ranger called Laboeuf. Laboeuf is of a different disposition to Mattie and Cogburn, with alternate plans for Chaney.<br /><br /><b>“Who is the best marshal they have?'<br /><br />The sheriff thought on it for a minute. He said, 'I would have to weigh that proposition. There is near about two hundred of them. I reckon William Waters is the best tracker. He is a half-breed Comanche and it is something to see, watching him cut for sign. The meanest one is Rooster Cogburn. He is a pitiless man, double-tough, and fear don't enter into his thinking. He loves to pull a cork. Now L.T. Quinn, he brings his prisoners in alive. He may let one get by now and then but he believes even the worst of men is entitled to a fair shake. Also the court does not pay any fees for dead men. Quinn is a good peace officer and a lay preacher to boot. He will not plant evidence or abuse a prisoner. He is straight as a string. Yes, I will say Quinn is about the best they have.'<br /><br />I said, 'Where can I find this Rooster?”</b><br /><br />In no time the three ride out on their quest to bring in the outlaw. I had never read a western before 2020, but this was my fourth and I just don’t know what it is about the genre but the writing is magical. There is a poetic and lyrical style to these stories, and especially in True Grit the dialogue is close to genius. It never feels overdone at all and the language is extremely compelling. It has an honesty to it that I have rarely found in other styles of storytelling. <br /><br />Also, it has plenty of revolvers and six-shooters and repeating rifles and horse rides over the plain. True Grit is packed with everyone’s favourite and typical western components and feels extraordinarily realistic. The relationship between the three justice-seekers is well-done, the description and scene setting is beautiful and honest and the plot is simple but very fun to read.<br /><br /><b>“You must pay for everything in this world one way and another. There is nothing free except the Grace of God. You cannot earn that or deserve it.”</b><br /><br />It is a short story at just over 230 pages long, I was able to read it in an evening. It is easy going and natural and I can see this being a feel-good read of mine for many years to come. If you’ve seen the film(s) of True Grit you’ll soon see that they are extremely faithful to the book. No wonder the films were so good if they had some awesome material to use. Both John Wayne and Jeff Bridges are sublime as Rooster Cogburn.<br /><br /><b>“That's bold talk for a one-eyed fat man.”<br /><br />5/5 - A superb story of justice, the resilience of a 14-year-old girl and cowboy classics. SO easy to read and so easy to enjoy, I would recommend this to everyone who reads! Cowboy hats off to Charles Portis.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="11670805394074163141" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_635259234" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/635259234" />
    <a title="Carol" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7668522-carol"><img alt="Carol" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546364878p2/7668522.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/635259234?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol" href="/user/show/7668522-carol">Carol</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/10035073-true-grit">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=western">western</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer7267098615632187076">I have always loved the old <b>True Grit</b> movie with John Wayne, and now I can say <b>I love the novel too!</b><p>This entertaining 1870's Wild West adventure begins when a formidable and funny 14 year old Mattie Ross hires the fat and fearless one-eyed Sheriff Rooster Cogburn to track down outlaw Tom Chaney who gunned down her beloved father in cold blood. With the assistance of a Texas Ranger and repeated threats of retaliation from Mattie's relentless lawyer Daggett for anyone who gets in her way, the cont</p></span>
  <span id="freeText7267098615632187076" style="display:none">I have always loved the old <b>True Grit</b> movie with John Wayne, and now I can say <b>I love the novel too!</b><p>This entertaining 1870's Wild West adventure begins when a formidable and funny 14 year old Mattie Ross hires the fat and fearless one-eyed Sheriff Rooster Cogburn to track down outlaw Tom Chaney who gunned down her beloved father in cold blood. With the assistance of a Texas Ranger and repeated threats of retaliation from Mattie's relentless lawyer Daggett for anyone who gets in her way, the contrary group embark on their journey.</p><p>Filled with many laughable and unforgettable one-liners, this delightful western classic will always be a favorite in my book! Highly recommend!</p></span>

  <span id="freeText11086272340916368507" style="display:none">My book is the 2010 reprint (with an afterward by Donna Tartt ) which coincided with the release of the the second True Grit movie directed by the Cohen Brothers and starring Jeff Bridges, but my memories of the story come straight from the great old John Wayne production in 1968, which most likely I watched on a black and white screen. Scrappy Mattie Ross is only 14 when she hires a beat up , mean , old one- eyed Marshall, Rooster Cogburn to help her avenge the robbery and death of her father by the cowardly Thomas Chaney, and then accompanies him into the wilderness of Indian territory to accomplish the task. Mattie's a girl with a lot of pluck who was hard to forget even after all these years. Glad I finally got around to reading this American Classic for the first time, even if it's getting close to 50 years later. 4 stars</span>

<div id="review_3208534483" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3208534483" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3208534483?book_show_action=true">Feb 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=american-classics">american-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=southern-lit">southern-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=2020-aty-challenge">2020-aty-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=borrowed-from-library">borrowed-from-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3208534483" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15309290679154298998">I have long meant to read this novel, and it is sad that the thing that finally got me to it was the death of the author. We lost Charles Portis on February 17, 2020. Sadder even than his death was knowing that he had suffered from Alzheimers for six years prior. Such a vicious disease that robs its victims of their very identity.<br/><br/>Mattie Ross from near Dardanelle in Yell County is one of the most memorable characters in Western literature. She is surrounded by other unforgettables, Rooster Cogbur</span>
  <span id="freeText15309290679154298998" style="display:none">I have long meant to read this novel, and it is sad that the thing that finally got me to it was the death of the author. We lost Charles Portis on February 17, 2020. Sadder even than his death was knowing that he had suffered from Alzheimers for six years prior. Such a vicious disease that robs its victims of their very identity.<br /><br />Mattie Ross from near Dardanelle in Yell County is one of the most memorable characters in Western literature. She is surrounded by other unforgettables, Rooster Cogburn and Texas Ranger LaBoeuf among them, but she is the heart and soul of this novel and while she is looking for true grit in a man to chase down her father’s killer, she is the person with true grit in this novel and that is unmistakable.<br /><br />It is a fast-paced novel that I swept through in one day. Of course, knowing the story beforehand is a double-edged sword, it makes the reading flow but it takes the surprise out of coming events. One of the things I dearly prized was Charles Portis’ sense of humor that is peppered throughout the book and made me chuckle aloud a time or two.<br /><br />Describing the sitting judge in Fort Smith: <i>On his deathbed he asked for a priest and became a Catholic. That was his wife’s religion. It was his own business and none of mine. If you had sentenced one hundred and sixty men to death and seen around eighty of them swing, then maybe at the last minute you would feel the need of some stronger medicine than the Methodists could make.</i><br /><br />Or wrangling with Mr. Stonehill, who gets the worst end of Mattie’s sharpness and persistence. <br /><br /><i>”Lawyer Daggett is the man who forced them into receivership,” said I. “They tried to ‘mess’ with him. It was a feather in his cap. He is on familiar terms with important men in Little Rock. The talk is he will be governor one day.” “Then he is a man of little ambition,” said Stonehill, “incommensurate with his capacity for making mischief.”<br /><br />I have seen both movie versions of True Grit and enjoyed them equally. A great movie based on a great book. Well done, Mr. Portis. <br /></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="15309290679154298998" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3505180158" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3505180158" />
    <a title="Elizabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/75748905-elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564237944p2/75748905.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3505180158?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth" href="/user/show/75748905-elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Elizabeth by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/5211606-julie">Julie</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75748905-elizabeth?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/75748905-elizabeth?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3505180158" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3976753559139335802">What was I doing when I was 14? Certainly not enlisting the help of mean U.S. Marshal Rooster Cogburn and Texas Ranger LaBoeuf to hunt down my father’s killer through the mountains and wilderness of Arkansas. At that age, I had… gone camping… I guess. Put me in Mattie’s shoes, and I would’ve floundered somewhere between haggling for the repayment of a stolen saddle and crossing a river on a horse. But if we placed Mattie squarely in 2020, I have no doubt she’d be chasing down the tails of every </span>
  <span id="freeText3976753559139335802" style="display:none">What was I doing when I was 14? Certainly not enlisting the help of mean U.S. Marshal Rooster Cogburn and Texas Ranger LaBoeuf to hunt down my father’s killer through the mountains and wilderness of Arkansas. At that age, I had… gone camping… I guess. Put me in Mattie’s shoes, and I would’ve floundered somewhere between haggling for the repayment of a stolen saddle and crossing a river on a horse. But if we placed Mattie squarely in 2020, I have no doubt she’d be chasing down the tails of every unjust and lawless scoundrel who did her wrong. At this point, she’d probably have dismantled our entire executive branch and some senators to boot. <br /><br />In other words, Mattie Ross of near Dardanelle, Arkansas is the spunky, no-nonsense girl protagonist that I never knew I needed to have in literature. Charles Portis has made me fall wholly in love with Mattie’s unflinching, ascetic, deadpan narration. She writes as a whip-smart and unsentimental spinster, recounting events decades earlier that transpired in 1873—how she chased after Tom Chaney, that vile man who shot her kind father and dashed off into Indian territory to avoid retribution. He would of course come to rue that decision, for little did he know that Mattie was a level-headed girl of 14 who would gladly enact retribution. <br /><br />Society might’ve called her an “unnatural child” who refused to wait patiently for the law to get its hat on straight, but Mattie could care less that Tom Chaney be punished for “how many dogs and fat men he killed in Texas.” No, he would pay for killing her father. The most unadorned writing is veritably teeming with the vitality of Mattie’s personality, unhumorous in tone but hilarious to read, never foolish in observation yet endearing towards the fools. <br /><br />Mattie has <i>true grit</i> in every sense of the word, with the fire of her determination stoked by sheer will and courage. She soldiers on through rugged travel conditions and complains little. We are along for a fast and breath-stealing ride into hostile territory headlined by this dynamic, bickering trio of Mattie, Cogburn, and LaBoeuf, chock full of classic Western ambushes and gunfights. To hear a relatively dry retelling in Mattie’s distinctive voice is uniquely delightful and nerve-wracking. This man fires his rifle, that man dodges, those people are shot! Snakes! Skeletons! She will add in utter solemnity that a “scrap did not last as long as it has taken me to describe it.” Just in case you improperly over-excite yourself about a shootout experienced by a young girl, right? <br /><br />Portis’s <i>True Grit</i> is a true gem in literature, and I am so glad that my particular friend Julie brought this classic to my attention. There are few classics where I can say, plain and simple, that I had such unadulterated <i>fun</i>. No overwrought angst and a compelling story to tell—my goodness, what silliness has plagued young adults since then, Mattie would lament! </span>
  <a data-text-id="3976753559139335802" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emma" href="/user/show/8575414-emma">Emma</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1690504332" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15223079688852982798">PEOPLE do not give it credence that a fourteen-year-old girl could leave home and go off in the wintertime to avenge her father’s blood but it did not seem so strange then, although I will say it did not happen every day. <br/><br/>This is the story of Mattie Ross from Dardanelles, Arkansas and what a story it is! I haven't read anything else by Charles Portis but the voice of his protagonist is absolutely authentic and credible. To listen to this young girl, so assured, so determined, browbeat all those </span>
  <span id="freeText15223079688852982798" style="display:none">PEOPLE do not give it credence that a fourteen-year-old girl could leave home and go off in the wintertime to avenge her father’s blood but it did not seem so strange then, although I will say it did not happen every day. <br /><br />This is the story of Mattie Ross from Dardanelles, Arkansas and what a story it is! I haven't read anything else by Charles Portis but the voice of his protagonist is absolutely authentic and credible. To listen to this young girl, so assured, so determined, browbeat all those who try to cheat her, manipulate, shortchange her in anyway is genius. She is a whirlwind of pure Will who has the complete measure of Rooster Cogburn and Le Boeuf, the two marshals who accompany her on her quest.<br /><br />Mattie selects Cogburn for this job because she judges him to have 'grit" but I am sure it won't be a surprise to anyone that it is in fact her who possess the True Grit of the title.<br /><br />A classic story which I strongly recommend to all.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15223079688852982798" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_975916544" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/975916544" />
    <a title="Howard" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32041201-howard"><img alt="Howard" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587918150p2/32041201.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/975916544?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Howard" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Howard" href="/user/show/32041201-howard">Howard</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=american-west">american-west</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=coming-of-age">coming-of-age</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=western-fiction">western-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=oklahoma">oklahoma</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=tall-woman">tall-woman</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=favorite">favorite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32041201-howard?shelf=arkansas">arkansas</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer975916544" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1333886310626588313"><b>"People do not give it credence that a fourteen-year-old girl could leave home and go off in the wintertime to avenge her father’s blood but it did not seem so strange then, although I will say it did not happen every day. I was just fourteen years of age when a coward going by the name of Tom Chaney shot my father down in Fort Smith, Arkansas, and robbed him of his life and his horse and $150 in cash money plus two California gold pieces that he carried in his trouser band."</b> <br/><br/>So begins Charles P</span>
  <span id="freeText1333886310626588313" style="display:none"><b>"People do not give it credence that a fourteen-year-old girl could leave home and go off in the wintertime to avenge her father’s blood but it did not seem so strange then, although I will say it did not happen every day. I was just fourteen years of age when a coward going by the name of Tom Chaney shot my father down in Fort Smith, Arkansas, and robbed him of his life and his horse and $150 in cash money plus two California gold pieces that he carried in his trouser band."</b> <br /><br />So begins Charles Portis’ 1968 novel,<b> True Grit</b>. Mattie Ross, now a forty-year-old spinster, narrates the events that surrounded her quest to find and punish her father’s killer. The opening passage demonstrates the deadpan quality of her narration as well as the detail in which she recounts her single-minded determination to achieve her goal no matter the obstacles that she will have to fight to overcome.<br /><br />Chaney, after killing her father, fled to the Indian Territory (present-day Oklahoma) and joined up with the “Lucky” Ned Pepper gang. Mattie realizes that she can’t travel alone into that treacherous territory and achieve her goal of bringing Chaney back to Fort Smith to stand trial in Judge Isaac Parker’s federal court for the Western District of Arkansas. That court also has jurisdiction over any case in the territory that involves a white person who is either a victim or perpetrator. Therefore, because the territory comes under federal jurisdiction, she sets out to hire the district’s meanest, toughest, orneriest U.S. deputy marshal to assist her.<br /><br />She seeks advice on this matter from the local sheriff:<br /><br /><b>“'Who is the best marshal they have?'”<br /><br />“The sheriff thought on it for a minute. He said, ‘I would have to weigh that proposition. There is near about two hundred of them. I reckon William Waters is the best tracker. He is a half-breed Comanche and it is something to see, watching him cast for sign. The meanest one is Rooster Cogburn. He is a pitiless man, double-tough, and fear don’t enter into his thinking. He loves to pull a cork. Now L.T. Quinn, he brings prisoners in alive. He may let one get by now and then but he believes even the worst of men is entitled to a fair shake. Also the court does not pay any fees for dead men. Quinn is a good peace officer and a lay preacher to boot. He will not plant evidence or abuse a prisoner. He is straight as a string. Yes, I will say Quinn is about the best they have.’”<br /><br />“I said, ‘Where can I find this Rooster?’”</b><br /><br />Mattie isn’t looking for a good tracker, or a fair man, she is looking for a man with “true grit,” a characteristic that she admires and that which she personally possesses in full measure. It turns out that Reuben J. “Rooster” Cogburn is a one-eyed, hard-drinking, ruthless, fat man of about forty who isn’t sure that he wants to work for Mattie, or, as he makes plain, any woman. But after much verbal sparring between the two, Rooster’s reticence is overcome by Mattie’s agreement to pay him the hundred dollars he demands for taking on the job. It is more than she wants to pay, but she compromises by promising to pay him fifty now and the other fifty after the mission is accomplished.<br /><br />Matters become even more complicated when a Texas Ranger by the name of LaBoeuf (pronounced La-Beef) arrives in Fort Smith. He is also on Chaney’s trail. It seems that Chaney killed a state senator in Texas and that there is a sizable bounty on his head. The marshal and the ranger decide to join forces and split the proceeds if they are able to capture – or kill – the fugitive.<br /><br /><b>“Anyhow, it sounds queer. Five hundred dollars is mighty little for a man that killed a senator.”<br /><br />“Bibbs was a little senator,” said LaBoeuf. “They would not have put up anything except it would look bad.”</b> <br /><br />Neither of the lawmen wants a fourteen-year-old girl to tag along and they attempt to leave her behind, but they don’t know Mattie. She will not be denied. The three, at odds with each other, and with differing goals, ride into the territory in search of Tom Chaney.<br /><br />Even if you have watched one or both of the movies based on the book, both of which are good adaptations, the book is still an enjoyable read. It is unfortunate that the two successful movies have had the effect of shoving the book below the reading public’s radar screen. However, the publisher did re-issue the book as a tie-in with the later movie and therefore it is back in print and is no longer hard to locate.<br /><br />Novelist Donna Tartt, writing in the introduction to the new edition, calls the book a masterpiece. She writes that four generations of her family, beginning with her great grandmother, deeply admired the novel. Her great grandmother was in her eighties when she first read it and introduced it to the other females in the family: her middle-aged grandmother; her twenty-something mother; and to her, who was ten when she first read it. <br /><br />She does not mention any male members of her family being enamored with the book, and it is easy to see how this independent, bold, courageous, and yes, self-righteous and unaware young heroine would resonate with her and her female relatives. Mattie is the star of the story, but she is ably assisted by Rooster and LaBeouf and there are enough thrills and adventures to appeal to readers regardless of gender or age.<br /><br />One of the book’s many qualities is that it can be read on more than one level. It can be approached as a coming-of-age story, or an adventure story, or a satire, or a story of redemption and loss of innocence, for it contains all these elements. As Michael Cleary wrote in Twentieth Century Western Writers, “<b>True Grit</b> is … a curious amalgam of parody, formula, and myth.” Cleary points out that Rooster, motivated by greed rather than justice, violates almost all perceptions of a Western hero. “Portis overlays realism on the romantic world of the West. [Therefore,] Rooster is not burdened by the moral introspection of a Virginian or Shane.” <br /><br />But Rooster meets his match when he tries to get the best of Mattie Ross. Here we have two people who are willing to do whatever is necessary to achieve their goals. But more times than not, it is Mattie who prevails. Mattie is not only smart and stubborn, she believes that others should carry out her wishes. Why? Well, because her self-assurance tells her without reservation that it is the right thing to do.<br /><br />Part of the appeal of the novel is that Mattie’s narration contains much deadpan humor. However, she doesn’t know that. She is unaware of how she sounds and, anyway, she wouldn’t care even if she did.<br /><br />Here are a couple examples of her unintentional humor:<br /><br /><b>“On his deathbed he asked for a priest and became a Catholic. That was his wife’s religion. It was his own business and none of mine. If you had sentenced one hundred and sixty men to death and seen around eighty of them swing, then maybe at the last minute you would feel the need for some stronger medicine than the Methodists could make.”<br /><br />“You can expect that out of Federal people and to make it worse this was a Republican gang that cared nothing for the opinion of the good people of Arkansas who are Democrats.</b>”<br /><br />Portis was born and raised in southern Arkansas, was educated at the University of Arkansas, and has lived most of his life in the state. That background allows him in <b>True Grit</b> to demonstrate his deep understanding of the people, place, and language of the time. In a profile of Portis in the New York Times, Charles McGrath writes, Portis “doesn’t use e-mail, has an unlisted phone number, declines interview requests … and shuns photographs with the ardor of a fugitive in the witness protection program.” Maybe that reluctance stems from the many years he spent as a reporter prior to becoming a full-time novelist and is aware of how interviewers sometimes misquote or misconstrue or otherwise distort the interviewee’s remarks. <br /><br />Portis is the author of five novels. <b>True Grit</b> was his second. The first was Norwood (1966), filmed after <b>True Grit</b>, it flopped at the box office. The other three are The Dog of the South(1979), Masters of Atlantis (1989), and Gringos (1991).</span>
  <a data-text-id="1333886310626588313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2478903833" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2478903833" />
    <a title="Ken" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/730754-ken"><img alt="Ken" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565570004p2/730754.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2478903833?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ken" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ken" href="/user/show/730754-ken">Ken</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/10035073-true-grit">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/730754-ken?shelf=finished-in-2018">finished-in-2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2478903833" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15791421947272338970">I was tempted to add this to my western shelf, but I don't HAVE a western shelf, because I don't read westerns. But <i>True Grit</i> is a pleasant surprise, a dark horse, a little page turner that could. <br/><br/>Quite simply, it's a classic mix of an upright, stubborn, and principled young girl out to avenge the murder of her father in post-Civil War Arkansas. She teams up with Rooster Cogburn, a wily old ask-no-questions marshal who brings them in more dead than alive, and a younger Texan dude named LaBoeuf (</span>
  <span id="freeText15791421947272338970" style="display:none">I was tempted to add this to my western shelf, but I don't HAVE a western shelf, because I don't read westerns. But <i>True Grit</i> is a pleasant surprise, a dark horse, a little page turner that could. <br /><br />Quite simply, it's a classic mix of an upright, stubborn, and principled young girl out to avenge the murder of her father in post-Civil War Arkansas. She teams up with Rooster Cogburn, a wily old ask-no-questions marshal who brings them in more dead than alive, and a younger Texan dude named LaBoeuf (pronounced La-Beef). The mix produces humor, terrific dialogue, and, of course, the adventure you'd expect in lawmen with a 14-yr-old chasing down posses of bad guys.<br /><br />But really, what makes this book work is voice. Mattie Ross's narration is wonderful to behold, as are the expressions of her day. The dialect is not as difficult as Twain's <i>Huckleberry Finn</i>, but comparisons are otherwise justified. It's a no-brainer for Twain fans to become Portis fans.<br /><br />I pulled this off of my classroom library shelf before skedaddling in late June. Glad I did. As for the classroom itself, it must be understood that, as with Twain, there are a few (MUCH fewer) un-PC references to blacks in the early section of the book, but the one black character (as with Twain again) is as positive as certain ions. Some "filler" parts in the traveling avengers chat-fest, but overall, high marks. And, as the afterword attests, this is Donna Tartt's favorite book.<br /><br />Also of note: I have never seen the John Wayne movie of this book, so I've no clue if that would affect my reading either way.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2109094799" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2109094799" />
    <a title="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes"><img alt="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598297811p2/1452829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2109094799?book_show_action=true">Aug 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~" href="/user/show/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes">Mary ~Ravager of Tomes~</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=year-2017">year-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1452829-mary-ravager-of-tomes?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2109094799" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10656951112944871338"><b>Actual Rating: 3.5 Stars</b><br/><br/>Young Mattie Ross, fourteen & with the fires of revenge in her eyes, sets off across the perilous native lands of Arkansas with a drunkard Marshal & a Texas bounty hunter to lay justice at the feet of her father's killer.<br/><br/><b>If this premise <i>sounds</i> badass, it's because it <u>absolutely is.</u></b><br/><br/>The best part of this novel is our narrator, Mattie Ross. She's fearless & armed with an <b>intimidating knowledge of Bible scripture.</b> Her story is spoken <b>without</b> the self-interest or manipulation </span>
  <span id="freeText10656951112944871338" style="display:none"><b>Actual Rating: 3.5 Stars</b><br /><br />Young Mattie Ross, fourteen &amp; with the fires of revenge in her eyes, sets off across the perilous native lands of Arkansas with a drunkard Marshal &amp; a Texas bounty hunter to lay justice at the feet of her father's killer.<br /><br /><b>If this premise <i>sounds</i> badass, it's because it <u>absolutely is.</u></b><br /><br />The best part of this novel is our narrator, Mattie Ross. She's fearless &amp; armed with an <b>intimidating knowledge of Bible scripture.</b> Her story is spoken <b>without</b> the self-interest or manipulation you may expect from an adult with a reputation to maintain. <br /><br />Mattie is unashamed to admit that retribution is her vice, <i>and your opinion on the matter is of no consequence one way or the other.</i><br /><br />The <b>clever but unadorned</b> language used to spin this tale really set the tone for a harsh Western winter in the 1870's. I was particularly fond of the way Portis wrote the interactions between characters; there's an <b>effortless authenticity</b> here that feels true to the time period even now 50 years after it was published. <br /><br /><b>So, why only a 3.5 stars?</b><br /><br />Honestly, it comes entirely down to personal preference here. <i>I don't know</i> for sure whether or not I am a fan of the Western genre, as I don't have a lot of experience with it. <br /><br /><u>When I think Western stereotypes, I think of the following:</u><br /> <br />✘ Dusty but suave male lead who has an easy time of getting women to sleep with him.<br /><br />✘ Horses.<br /><br />✘ A scene in a bar (with optional bar fight.)<br /><br />✘ HORSES!<br /><br />✘ A scene in a jail.<br /><br />✘ <i>Neighhhhhhhh</i><br /><br />✘ Showdown/duel style finale.<br /><br /><b>This isn't a narrative I can get excited about.</b><br /><br />However, when I first watched Tarantino's <i>Django Unchained,</i> I was struck with an instalove strong enough to rival <b>even the shittiest</b> of Young Adult novels.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>Here was the type of Western I didn't know I needed in my life.</i>
</b><br /><br />Ever since, I've had a tough time not comparing my Western endeavors across all mediums to that film. As far as this book is concerned, <b>it actually measures up quite well.</b> I love the characters &amp; I think it definitely delivers the quality story promised in the premise.<br /><br /><b>It just didn't WOW me.</b> I enjoyed it, but I won't be choosing this book to accompany me on a deserted island.<br /><br />I'm glad to have finally read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/257845.True_Grit" title="True Grit by Charles Portis" rel="nofollow noopener">True Grit</a>, as I think it's <u>very much worth reading,</u> and I'm even more glad that now I can watch both the 1969 and 2010 adaptions with a clear conscience!<br /><br /><i><a href="http://booknest.eu/reviews/34-mary-s-reviews/770-truegrit" rel="nofollow noopener">This review</a> and other reviews of mine can be found on <a href="http://booknest.eu/" rel="nofollow noopener">Book Nest</a>!</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="10656951112944871338" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2605518706?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Britany" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Britany" href="/user/show/5550397-britany">Britany</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781590204597" href="/book/show/9435127-true-grit">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5550397-britany?shelf=books-to-film">books-to-film</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5550397-britany?shelf=2018">2018</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2605518706" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3644906919134422799">I needed a western to read for a challenge prompt and that is so far outside of my comfort zone, when a GR friend recommended this one. <br/><br/>True Grit was such a wonderfully told story, completely punched me in the gut towards the end and will be one that I will pay it forward and recommend to other friends to read. Mattie Ross is 14 and is ready to avenge her father's murder by Tom Chaney. She goes off to find a marshal with grit and meets Rooster Cogburn. Another Texas Ranger is also hunting for Mr</span>
  <span id="freeText3644906919134422799" style="display:none">I needed a western to read for a challenge prompt and that is so far outside of my comfort zone, when a GR friend recommended this one. <br /><br />True Grit was such a wonderfully told story, completely punched me in the gut towards the end and will be one that I will pay it forward and recommend to other friends to read. Mattie Ross is 14 and is ready to avenge her father's murder by Tom Chaney. She goes off to find a marshal with grit and meets Rooster Cogburn. Another Texas Ranger is also hunting for Mr Chaney as he shot a Texas Senator and his dog. These three unlikely companions ride off into the Oklahoma country to search for Ned Pepper and his band of robbers. <br /><br />This is exactly the type of western I wanted. Mattie Ross is a smart and determined girl. One I think we all wish we could be at 14. She goes after what she wants (sometimes putting herself in scary situations) but her bravery and spunk pull her forward. The book ends in a culmination of events that left me winded and worried. My heart broke at one point <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ BLACKIE!!!!!!! That poor pony- is this where "salt in the wound" came from?? I hope not!  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> but westerns certainly can't be all rainbows and glitter. I loved Mattie's perspective and the choices the author took to create this strong character and looking back at her life to tell us this story. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3644906919134422799" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2455916184" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2455916184" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2455916184?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/25512108-true-grit">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2455916184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12150053490344534519"><b>3.5 stars</b><br/><br/>Because this is a well-loved classic, and especially because it is well-loved by some of my closest Goodreads friends, I feel I need to qualify my stars by saying, yes, I did like it. If I didn’t “really like” it, that’s merely because I get tired of ‘action’ rather quickly: as harrowing as Mattie’s last dilemma was, for me it went on too long (I know I have to be in the minority on that). I enjoyed the verbal interplay of the characters and the deadpan voice of Mattie, a woman looking </span>
  <span id="freeText12150053490344534519" style="display:none"><b>3.5 stars</b><br /><br />Because this is a well-loved classic, and especially because it is well-loved by some of my closest Goodreads friends, I feel I need to qualify my stars by saying, yes, I did like it. If I didn’t “really like” it, that’s merely because I get tired of ‘action’ rather quickly: as harrowing as Mattie’s last dilemma was, for me it went on too long (I know I have to be in the minority on that). I enjoyed the verbal interplay of the characters and the deadpan voice of Mattie, a woman looking back on what happened to her fourteen-year-old self a quarter-of-a-century ago. The matter-of-fact attitude toward greed, lawlessness and violence may help toward explaining the United States of today.<br /><br />In the afterword <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8719.Donna_Tartt" title="Donna Tartt" rel="nofollow noopener">Donna Tartt</a> writes of her love for the book and how that love was passed down to her by its starting with her gentle, innocent, elderly great-grandmother. It wouldn’t be wrong to say the novel became a sort of family Bible—if only for the females, as she doesn’t mention any male relatives—and that Tartt knows it by heart. All very interesting to me, as when I was reading the account of Mattie’s grapples with her enemy, the violent struggle of Harriet with her nemesis in Tartt’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/775346.The_Little_Friend" title="The Little Friend by Donna Tartt" rel="nofollow noopener">The Little Friend</a> came immediately to mind. Now that I’ve read this, I know Harriet’s (literary) ancestor.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12150053490344534519" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12453325760991647309">I picked up this novel in part because of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1572887853' rel='nofollow noopener'> Warwick's great review </a> and I was not disappointed. <br/><br/>This addictively readable story is part comedy, part character study, part adventure, and part history lesson. I listened to the audio version narrated by Donna Tartt who is pitch-perfect as Maddie Ross. The audio edition also includes an interesting essay written by Tartt that looks at <i>True Grit</i>'s appeal across multiple generations and discusses some of the reasons why it's so freakin' good. </span>
  <span id="freeText12453325760991647309" style="display:none">I picked up this novel in part because of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1572887853" rel="nofollow noopener"> Warwick's great review </a> and I was not disappointed. <br /><br />This addictively readable story is part comedy, part character study, part adventure, and part history lesson. I listened to the audio version narrated by Donna Tartt who is pitch-perfect as Maddie Ross. The audio edition also includes an interesting essay written by Tartt that looks at <i>True Grit</i>'s appeal across multiple generations and discusses some of the reasons why it's so freakin' good. </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('KyMtulDrqSYQxHMzwqskq/1RKOzQRuslmYi5EqtPna+MgMJlQKn997D02NrNH+KwvSEekccXkmgC0rgdPyk9dQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('XjFz0tSghX2VzOMxHCp5n2KtqLv920HbI0lhbHJmjzj5kpwNxOLRrDX8SNgTnr+EIt2exuqKOJa4E2Bj5gAv4g==')})
  }

Full of wit and psychological poignancy, A Fairly Good Time, here with Green Water, Green Sky, encapsulates Gallant’s unparalleled skill as a storyteller. Shirley Perrigny (née Norrington, then briefly Higgins), the heroine of A Fairly Good Time, is an original. Derided by the Parisians she lives among and chided by her fellow Canadians, this young widow—recently remarried to a French journalist named Philippe—is fond of quoting Jane Austen and Kingsley Amis and of using her myopia as a defense against social aggression. As the fixed points in Shirley’s life begin to recede—Philippe having apparently though not definitively left—her freewheeling, makeshift, and self-abnegating ways come to seem an aspect of devotion to her fellow man. Could this unreliable protagonist be the unwitting heroine of her own story? Green Water, Green Sky, Gallant’s first novel, is a darker tale of the fractured family life of Bonnie McCarthy, an American divorcée, and her daughter, Flor. Uprooted and unmoored, mother and daughter live like itinerants—in Venice, Cannes, and Paris—glamorous and dependent. <span id="freeTextContainer17148052949526928398">Charles McColl Portis was an American author best known for his novels <i>Norwood</i> (1966) and the classic Western <i>True Grit</i> (1968), both adapted as films. The latter also inspired a film sequel and a made-for-TV movie sequel. A newer film adaptation of <i>True Grit</i> was released in 2010. <br/><br/>Portis served in the Marine Corps during the Korean war and attended the University of Arkansas at Fayetteville. He gra</span>
  <span id="freeText17148052949526928398" style="display:none">Charles McColl Portis was an American author best known for his novels <i>Norwood</i> (1966) and the classic Western <i>True Grit</i> (1968), both adapted as films. The latter also inspired a film sequel and a made-for-TV movie sequel. A newer film adaptation of <i>True Grit</i> was released in 2010. <br /><br />Portis served in the Marine Corps during the Korean war and attended the University of Arkansas at Fayetteville. He graduated with a degree in journalism in 1958.<br /><br />His journalistic career included work at the <i>Arkansas Gazette</i> before he moved to New York to work for <i> The New York Herald Tribune</i>. After serving as the London bureau chief for the <i> The New York Herald Tribune</i>, he left journalism in 1964 and returned to Arkansas to write novels.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17148052949526928398" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
